nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/&#618;&#601;r/: \'ear\' in \'near\'">&#618;&#601;r</span><span title="/o&#650;/: \'o\' in \'code\'">o&#650;</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>: <i>Nero Claudius Caesar Augustus Germanicus</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[i]</a></sup> 15 December 37 &#8211; 9 June 68 AD) was the last <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Julio-Claudian_dynasty" title="Julio-Claudian dynasty">Julio-Claudian dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jarus_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jarus-2">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History.com_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-History.com-3">[2]</a></sup> He was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adoption_in_Ancient_Rome" title="Adoption in Ancient Rome">adopted by</a> his great-uncle <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> and became Claudius\' heir and successor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jarus_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jarus-2">[1]</a></sup> Like Claudius, Nero became emperor with the consent of the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guard</a>. Nero\'s mother, <a href="/wiki/Agrippina_the_Younger" title="Agrippina the Younger">Agrippina the Younger</a>, was likely implicated in Claudius\' death and Nero\'s nomination as emperor. She dominated Nero\'s early life and decisions until he cast her off. Five years into his reign, he had her murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jarus_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jarus-2">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the early years of his reign, Nero was content to be guided by his mother, his tutor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seneca_the_younger" title="Seneca the younger">Lucius Annaeus Seneca</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefect" title="Praetorian prefect">Praetorian prefect</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sextus_Afranius_Burrus" title="Sextus Afranius Burrus">Sextus Afranius Burrus</a>. As time passed, he started to play a more active and independent role in government and foreign policy. During his reign, the redoubtable general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corbulo" title="Corbulo">Corbulo</a> conducted a <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Parthian_War_of_58%E2%80%9363" title="Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 58&#8211;63">successful war</a> and negotiated peace with the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>. His general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suetonius_Paulinus" title="Suetonius Paulinus">Suetonius Paulinus</a> crushed a major revolt in Britain, led by the <a href="/wiki/Iceni" title="Iceni">Iceni</a> Queen <a href="/wiki/Boudica" title="Boudica">Boudica</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bosporan_Kingdom" title="Bosporan Kingdom">Bosporan Kingdom</a> was briefly annexed to the empire, and the <a href="/wiki/First_Jewish%E2%80%93Roman_War" title="First Jewish&#8211;Roman War">First Jewish&#8211;Roman War</a> began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> Nero focused much of his attention on diplomacy, trade and the cultural life of the empire, ordering theatres built and promoting athletic games. He made public appearances as an actor, poet, musician and charioteer. In the eyes of traditionalists, this undermined the dignity and authority of his person, status, and office. His extravagant, empire-wide program of public and private works was funded by a rise in taxes that was much resented by the middle and upper classes. Various plots against his life were revealed; the ringleaders, most of them Nero\'s own courtiers, were executed.\n</p><p>In 68 AD <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vindex" title="Vindex">Vindex</a>, governor of the <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaulish</a> territory <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Lugdunensis" title="Gallia Lugdunensis">Gallia Lugdunensis</a>, rebelled. He was supported by <a href="/wiki/Galba" title="Galba">Galba</a>, the governor of <a href="/wiki/Hispania_Tarraconensis" title="Hispania Tarraconensis">Hispania Tarraconensis</a>. Vindex\'s revolt failed in its immediate aim, but Nero fled Rome when Rome\'s discontented civil and military authorities chose Galba as emperor. He committed suicide on June 9, 68 AD, when he learned that he had been <a href="/wiki/Trial_in_absentia" title="Trial in absentia">tried <i>in absentia</i></a> and condemned to death as a public enemy, making him the first Roman Emperor to commit suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> His death ended the Julio-Claudian dynasty, sparking a brief period of civil wars known as the <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Four_Emperors" title="Year of the Four Emperors">Year of the Four Emperors</a>.\n</p><p>Nero\'s rule is usually associated with <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyranny</a> and extravagance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> Most Roman sources, such as <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a>, offer overwhelmingly negative assessments of his personality and reign; <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> claims that the Roman people thought him compulsive and corrupt. Suetonius tells that many Romans believed that the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Rome" title="Great Fire of Rome">Great Fire of Rome</a> was instigated by Nero to clear the way for his planned palatial complex, the <a href="/wiki/Domus_Aurea" title="Domus Aurea">Domus Aurea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> According to Tacitus he was said to have seized Christians as scapegoats for the fire and burned them alive, seemingly motivated not by public justice but by personal cruelty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annals-xv-44_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-annals-xv-44-9">[8]</a></sup> Some modern historians question the reliability of the ancient sources on Nero\'s tyrannical acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> A few sources paint Nero in a more favorable light. There is evidence of his popularity among the Roman commoners, especially in the eastern provinces of the Empire, where a popular legend arose that Nero had not died and would return. At least three leaders of short-lived, failed rebellions presented themselves as "<a href="/wiki/Nero_Redivivus_legend" title="Nero Redivivus legend">Nero reborn</a>" to enlist popular support.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Early_life"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Early life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Nero\'s_reign_(54_AD&#8211;68_AD)"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Nero\'s reign (54 AD&#8211;68 AD)</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Early_reign"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Decline"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Decline</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Later years</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#The_revolt_of_Vindex_and_Galba_and_the_death_of_Nero"><span class="tocnumber">2.7</span> <span class="toctext">After Nero</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Military_conflicts"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Military conflicts</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Boudicca\'s_uprising"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Boudicca\'s uprising</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Peace_with_Parthia"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Peace with Parthia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#The_First_Jewish_War"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">The First Jewish War</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Pursuits"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Pursuits</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Historiography"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Nero_in_Jewish_and_Christian_tradition"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Nero in Jewish and Christian tradition</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Jewish_tradition"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Jewish tradition</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Christian_tradition"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Christian tradition</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-19"><a href="#Martyrdoms_of_Peter_and_Paul"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Martyrdoms of Peter and Paul</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-20"><a href="#The_Antichrist"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">The Antichrist</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Ancestry"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Ancestry</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Nero was born Lucius Domitius Ahenobarbus on 15 December 37<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antium" title="Antium">Antium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEGR_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-OEGR-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collins_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-collins-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>87</span></sup> He was the only son of <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Domitius_Ahenobarbus_(consul_32)" title="Gnaeus Domitius Ahenobarbus (consul 32)">Gnaeus Domitius Ahenobarbus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agrippina_the_Younger" title="Agrippina the Younger">Agrippina the Younger</a>. His maternal grandparents were <a href="/wiki/Germanicus" title="Germanicus">Germanicus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agrippina_the_Elder" title="Agrippina the Elder">Agrippina the Elder</a>; his mother, <a href="/wiki/Caligula" title="Caligula">Caligula</a>\'s sister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrett_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-barrett-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>5</span></sup> He was <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>\' great-great grandson, descended from the first Emperor\'s only daughter, <a href="/wiki/Julia_the_Elder" title="Julia the Elder">Julia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malitz_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-malitz-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2</span></sup>\n</p><p>The ancient biographer <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a>, who was critical of Nero\'s ancestors, wrote that Augustus had reproached Nero\'s grandfather for his unseemly enjoyment of violent <a href="/wiki/Gladiator" title="Gladiator">gladiator</a> games. According to J&#252;rgen Malitz, Suetonius tells that Nero\'s father was known to be "irascible and brutal", and that both "enjoyed chariot races and theater performances to a degree not befitting their position."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malitz_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-malitz-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup>\n</p><p>Nero\'s father, Domitius, died in 40. A few years before his death, Domitius had been involved in a political scandal that, according to Malitz, "could have cost him his life if <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> had not died in the year 37."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malitz_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-malitz-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup> In the previous year, Nero\'s mother Agrippina had been caught up in a scandal of her own. Caligula\'s beloved sister <a href="/wiki/Julia_Drusilla" title="Julia Drusilla">Drusilla</a> had recently died and Caligula began to feel threatened by his brother-in-law <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aemilius_Lepidus" title="Marcus Aemilius Lepidus">Marcus Aemilius Lepidus</a>. Agrippina, suspected of adultery with her brother-in-law, was forced to carry the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Funerary_urn" title="Funerary urn">funerary urn</a> after Lepidus\' execution. Caligula then banished his two surviving sisters, Agrippina and <a href="/wiki/Julia_Livilla" title="Julia Livilla">Julia Livilla</a>, to a remote island in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malitz_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-malitz-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>4</span></sup> According to <i>The Oxford Encyclopedia of Ancient Greece and Rome</i>, Agrippina was exiled for plotting to overthrow Caligula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEGR_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-OEGR-11">[10]</a></sup> Nero\'s inheritance was taken from him and he was sent to live with his paternal aunt <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domitia_Lepida" title="Domitia Lepida">Domitia Lepida</a>, the mother of <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a>\' third wife <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valeria_Messalina" title="Valeria Messalina">Valeria Messalina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shotter_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-shotter-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>11</span></sup>\n</p><p>Caligula\'s reign lasted from 37 until 41<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shotter_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-shotter-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>11</span></sup> He died from multiple stab wounds in January of 41 after being ambushed by his own <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guard</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Hill" title="Palatine Hill">Palatine Hill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caligula_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-caligula-16">[15]</a></sup> Claudius succeeded Caligula as Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caligula_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-caligula-16">[15]</a></sup> Agrippina married Claudius in 49<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span> and became his fourth wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[ii]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEGR_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-OEGR-11">[10]</a></sup> By February 49, she had persuaded Claudius to adopt her son Nero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[iii]</a></sup> After Nero\'s adoption, "Claudius" became part of his name: Nero Claudius Caesar Drusus Germanicus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[iv]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emperor_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-emperor-20">[16]</a></sup> Claudius had gold coins issued to mark the adoption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blackwell_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-blackwell-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>119</span></sup>  Classics professor <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Josiah_Osgood&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Josiah Osgood (page does not exist)">Josiah Osgood</a> has written that "the coins, through their distribution and imagery alike, showed that a new Leader was in the making."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osgood_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-osgood-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>231</span></sup> David Shotter noted that, despite events in Rome, Nero\'s step-brother <a href="/wiki/Britannicus" title="Britannicus">Britannicus</a> was more prominent in provincial coinages during the early 50s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emperor_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-emperor-20">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>52</span></sup>\n</p><p>Nero officially formally entered public life as an adult in 51<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>&#8212;he was around 14 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emperor_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-emperor-20">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>51</span></sup> When he turned 16, Nero married Claudius\' daughter (his own step-sister), <a href="/wiki/Claudia_Octavia" title="Claudia Octavia">Claudia Octavia</a>. Between the years 51<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span> and 53<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>, he gave several speeches on behalf of various communities including the Ilians; the <a href="/wiki/Apamea_(Phrygia)" title="Apamea (Phrygia)">Apameans</a>, requesting a five-year tax reprieve after an earthquake; and the northern colony of <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a>, after their settlement suffered a devastating fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osgood_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-osgood-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>231</span></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nero_Agrippina_aureus_54.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="358" data-file-width="729" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Nero_Agrippina_aureus_54.png/220px-Nero_Agrippina_aureus_54.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Nero_Agrippina_aureus_54.png/330px-Nero_Agrippina_aureus_54.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Nero_Agrippina_aureus_54.png/440px-Nero_Agrippina_aureus_54.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nero_Agrippina_aureus_54.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Aureus" title="Aureus">aureus</a> of Nero and his mother, <a href="/wiki/Agrippina_the_Younger" title="Agrippina the Younger">Agrippina</a>, c. 54</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NeroandClaudius.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="255" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/NeroandClaudius.jpg/220px-NeroandClaudius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/NeroandClaudius.jpg/330px-NeroandClaudius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/NeroandClaudius.jpg/440px-NeroandClaudius.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NeroandClaudius.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin issued under <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> celebrating young Nero as the future emperor, c. 50</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> died in 54<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>; many ancient historians claim that he was poisoned by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agrippina_the_younger" title="Agrippina the younger">Agrippina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup> Shotter has written that "Claudius\' death in 54<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span> has usually been regarded as an event hastened by Agrippina because of signs that Claudius was showing a renewed affection for his natural son," but he notes that among ancient sources <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a> was uniquely reserved in describing the poisoning as a rumor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emperor_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-emperor-20">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>53</span></sup> Contemporary sources differ in their accounts. Tacitus says that <a href="/wiki/Locusta" title="Locusta">Locusta</a> prepared the poison, which was served to the Emperor by his food taster <a href="/wiki/Halotus" title="Halotus">Halotus</a>. Tacitus also writes that Agrippina arranged for Claudius\' doctor <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Stertinius_Xenophon" title="Gaius Stertinius Xenophon">Xenophon</a> to administer poison, in the event that the Emperor survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emperor_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-emperor-20">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>53</span></sup> Suetonius differs in some details, but also implicates Halotus and Agrippina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[v]</a></sup> Like Tacitus, Cassius Dio writes that the poison was prepared by Locusta, but in Dio\'s account it is administered by Agrippina instead of Halotus. In <a href="/wiki/Apocolocyntosis" title="Apocolocyntosis">Apocolocyntosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca the Younger</a> does not mention mushrooms at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emperor_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-emperor-20">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>54</span></sup> Agrippina\'s involvement in Claudius\' death is not accepted by all modern scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>589</span></sup>\n</p><p>Before Claudius\' death, Agrippina had maneuvered to remove Britannicus\' tutors and replace them with tutors she had selected. She was also able to convince Claudius to replace with a single commander, <a href="/wiki/Sextus_Afranius_Burrus" title="Sextus Afranius Burrus">Burrus</a>, two prefects of the Praetorian guard who were suspected of supporting Brittanicus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shotter_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-shotter-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>13</span></sup> Since Agrippina had replaced the guard officers with men loyal to her, Nero was able to assume power without incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEGR_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-OEGR-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cambridge_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-cambridge-27">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>417</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Nero.27s_reign_.2854_AD.E2.80.9368_AD.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nero\'s_reign_(54_AD&#8211;68_AD)">Nero\'s reign (54 AD&#8211;68 AD)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Nero\'s reign (54 AD&#8211;68 AD)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Most of what we know about Nero\'s reign comes from three ancient writers: <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a>, and Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-griffin_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-griffin-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>37</span></sup>\n</p><p>According to ancient historians, Nero\'s construction projects were overly extravagant and the large number of expenditures under Nero left Italy "thoroughly exhausted by contributions of money" with "the provinces ruined."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[25]</a></sup> Modern historians, though, note that the period was riddled with deflation and that it is likely that Nero\'s spending came in the form of public-works projects and charity intended to ease economic troubles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_reign">Early reign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early reign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Nero_as_a_boy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5184" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Statue_of_Nero_as_a_boy.jpg/200px-Statue_of_Nero_as_a_boy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Statue_of_Nero_as_a_boy.jpg/300px-Statue_of_Nero_as_a_boy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Statue_of_Nero_as_a_boy.jpg/400px-Statue_of_Nero_as_a_boy.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Nero_as_a_boy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Nero as a boy</div></div></div>\n<p>Nero became emperor in 54 <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>, aged sixteen years.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span> This made him the youngest sole emperor until <a href="/wiki/Elagabalus" title="Elagabalus">Elagabalus</a>, who became emperor aged 14 in 218.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-32">[27]</a></sup>  The first five years of Nero\'s reign were described as <i>Quinquennium Neronis</i> by <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a>; the interpretation of the phrase is a matter of dispute amongst scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malitz_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-malitz-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>17</span></sup> As Pharaoh of Egypt, Nero adopted the royal titulary <i>Autokrator Neron Heqaheqau Meryasetptah Tjemaahuikhasut Wernakhtubaqet Heqaheqau Setepennenu Merur</i> ("Emperor Nero, Ruler of rulers, chosen by Ptah, beloved of Isis, the sturdy-armed one who struck the foreign lands, victorious for Egypt, ruler of rulers, chosen of Nun who loves him").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nero\'s tutor, Seneca, prepared Nero\'s first speech before the Senate. During this speech, Nero spoke about "eliminating the ills of the previous regime".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malitz_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-malitz-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>16</span></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H.H._Scullard" title="H.H. Scullard">H.H. Scullard</a> writes that "he promised to follow the Augustan model in his principate, to end all secret trials <i>intra cubiculum</i>, to have done with the corruption of court favorites and freedmen, and above all to respect the privileges of the Senate and individual Senators."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scullard_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-scullard-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>257</span></sup> His respect of the Senatorial autonomy, which distinguished him from Caligula and Claudius, was generally well received by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Roman Senate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malitz_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-malitz-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>18</span></sup>\n</p><p>Scullard writes that Nero\'s mother, Agrippina, "meant to rule through her son."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scullard_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-scullard-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>257</span></sup> Agrippina murdered her political rivals: Domitia Lepida, the aunt that Nero had lived with during Agrippina\'s exile; <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Junius_Silanus_(consul_46)" title="Marcus Junius Silanus (consul 46)">Marcus Junius Silanus</a>, a great grandson of Augustus; and <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_Claudius_Narcissus" title="Tiberius Claudius Narcissus">Narcissus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scullard_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-scullard-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>257</span></sup> One of the earliest coins that Nero issues during his reign shows Agrippina on the coin\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obverse" title="Obverse">obverse</a> side; usually, this would be reserved for a portrait of the emperor. The Senate also allowed Agrippina two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lictors" title="Lictors">lictors</a> during public appearances, an honor that was customarily bestowed upon only magistrates and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vestalis_Maxima" title="Vestalis Maxima">Vestalis Maxima</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malitz_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-malitz-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>16</span></sup> In <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>55, Nero removed Agrippina\'s ally <a href="/wiki/Pallas_(freedman)" title="Pallas (freedman)">Marcus Antonius Pallas</a> from his position in the treasury. Shotter writes the following about Agrippina\'s deteriorating relationship with Nero: "What Seneca and Burrus probably saw as relatively harmless in Nero&#8212;his cultural pursuits and his affair with the slave girl <a href="/wiki/Claudia_Acte" title="Claudia Acte">Claudia Acte</a>&#8212;were to her signs of her son\'s dangerous emancipation of himself from her influence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shotter_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-shotter-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>12</span></sup> Britannicus was poisoned after Agrippina threatened to side with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shotter_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-shotter-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>12</span></sup> Nero, who was having an affair with Acte,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[vi]</a></sup> exiled Agrippina from the palace when she began to cultivate a relationship with his wife Octavia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scullard_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-scullard-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>257</span></sup>\n</p><p>J&#252;rgen Malitz writes that ancient sources do not provide any clear evidence to evaluate the extent of Nero\'s personal involvement in politics during the first years of his reign. He describes the policies that are explicitly attributed to Nero as "well-meant but incompetent notions" like Nero\'s failed initiative to abolish taxes in 58<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>. Scholars generally credit Nero\'s advisors Burrus and Seneca with the administrative successes of these years. Malitz writes that in later years, Nero panicked when he had to make decisions on his own during times of crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malitz_14-8"><a href="#cite_note-malitz-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>19</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Matricide">Matricide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Matricide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nero_and_Poppaea_Sabina.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Nero_and_Poppaea_Sabina.jpg/220px-Nero_and_Poppaea_Sabina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Nero_and_Poppaea_Sabina.jpg/330px-Nero_and_Poppaea_Sabina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Nero_and_Poppaea_Sabina.jpg/440px-Nero_and_Poppaea_Sabina.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nero_and_Poppaea_Sabina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin of Nero and <a href="/wiki/Poppaea_Sabina" title="Poppaea Sabina">Poppaea Sabina</a></div></div></div>\n<p><i>The Oxford Encyclopedia of Ancient Greece and Rome</i> cautiously notes that Nero\'s reasons for killing his mother in 59<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span> are "not fully understood."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEGR_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-OEGR-11">[10]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>, the source of conflict between Nero and his mother was Nero\'s affair with <a href="/wiki/Poppaea_Sabina" title="Poppaea Sabina">Poppaea Sabina</a>. In <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Tacitus)" title="Histories (Tacitus)">Histories</a></i> Tacitus writes that the affair began while Poppaea was still married to <a href="/wiki/Rufrius_Crispinus" title="Rufrius Crispinus">Rufrius Crispinus</a>, but in his later work <i><a href="/wiki/Annals_(Tacitus)" title="Annals (Tacitus)">Annals</a></i> Tacitus says Poppaea was married to <a href="/wiki/Otho" title="Otho">Otho</a> when the affair began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrett_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-barrett-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>214</span></sup> In <i>Annals</i> Tacitus writes that Agrippina opposed Nero\'s affair with Poppaea because of her affection for his wife <a href="/wiki/Claudia_Octavia" title="Claudia Octavia">Octavia</a>. Anthony Barrett writes that Tacitus\' account in <i>Annals</i> "suggests that Poppaea\'s challenge drove [Nero] over the brink."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrett_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-barrett-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>215</span></sup> A number of modern historians have noted that Agrippina\'s death would not have offered much advantage for Poppaea, as Nero did not marry Poppaea until 62<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrett_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-barrett-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>215</span></sup> Barrett writes that Poppaea seems to serve as a "literary device, utilized [by Tacitus] because [he] could see no plausible explanation for Nero\'s conduct and also incidentally [served] to show that Nero, like Claudius, had fallen under the malign influence of a woman."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barrett_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-barrett-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>215</span></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a>, Nero had his former freedman <a href="/wiki/Anicetus_(admiral)" title="Anicetus (admiral)">Anicetus</a> arrange a shipwreck; Agrippina survived the wreck, swam ashore and was executed by Anicetus, who reported her death as a suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEGR_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-OEGR-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline">Decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Modern scholars believe that Nero\'s reign had been going well in the years before Agrippina\'s death. For example, Nero promoted the exploration of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_river" title="Nile river">Nile river</a> sources with a <a href="/wiki/Nero_exploration_of_Nile_river" title="Nero exploration of Nile river">successful expedition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[32]</a></sup> After Agrippina\'s exile, Burrus and Seneca were responsible for the administration of the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scullard_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-scullard-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>258</span></sup> However, Nero\'s "conduct became far more egregious" after his mother\'s death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEGR_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-OEGR-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>22</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Miriam_T._Griffin" title="Miriam T. Griffin">Miriam T. Griffins</a> suggests that Nero\'s decline began as early as 55<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span> with the murder of his stepbrother Britannicus, but also notes that "Nero lost all sense of right and wrong and listened to flattery with total credulity" after Agrippina\'s death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-griffin_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-griffin-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>84</span></sup> Griffin points out that Tacitus "makes explicit the significance of Agrippina\'s removal for Nero\'s conduct".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-griffin_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-griffin-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>84</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 62<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>, Nero\'s adviser <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burrus" title="Burrus">Burrus</a> died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEGR_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-OEGR-11">[10]</a></sup> That same year Nero called for the first treason trial of his reign (<i>maiestas</i> trial) against <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Antistius_Sosianus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Antistius Sosianus (page does not exist)">Antistius Sosianus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-griffin_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-griffin-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>53</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[34]</a></sup> He also executed his rivals <a href="/wiki/Faustus_Cornelius_Sulla_Felix" title="Faustus Cornelius Sulla Felix">Cornelius Sulla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rubellius_Plautus" title="Rubellius Plautus">Rubellius Plautus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malitz_14-9"><a href="#cite_note-malitz-14">[13]</a></sup> J&#252;rgen Malitz considers this to be a turning point in Nero\'s relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Roman Senate</a>. Malitz writes that "Nero abandoned the restraint he had previously shown because he believed a course supporting the Senate promised to be less and less profitable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malitz_14-10"><a href="#cite_note-malitz-14">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Burrus\' death, Nero appointed two new Praetorian Prefects: <a href="/wiki/Faenius_Rufus" title="Faenius Rufus">Faenius Rufus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ofonius_Tigellinus" title="Ofonius Tigellinus">Ofonius Tigellinus</a>. Politically isolated, Seneca was forced to retire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scullard_34-5"><a href="#cite_note-scullard-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>26</span></sup> According to Tacitus, Nero divorced Octavia on grounds of infertility, and banished her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-griffin_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-griffin-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>99</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annals-xiv-60_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-annals-xiv-60-41">[35]</a></sup> After public protests over Octavia\'s exile, Nero accused her of adultery with Anicetus and she was executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-griffin_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-griffin-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>99</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 64<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>, Nero married <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras_(freedman)" title="Pythagoras (freedman)">Pythagoras</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Freedman" title="Freedman">freedman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suetonius_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suetonius-43">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dio_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dio-44">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umich_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-umich-45">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Champlin146_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Champlin146-46">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Fire_of_Rome">Great Fire of Rome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Great Fire of Rome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Rome" title="Great Fire of Rome">Great Fire of Rome</a></div>\n<p>The Great Fire of Rome erupted on the night of 18 July to 19 July, <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">AD</a> 64. The fire started on the slope of the <a href="/wiki/Aventine_Hill" title="Aventine Hill">Aventine</a> overlooking the <a href="/wiki/Circus_Maximus" title="Circus Maximus">Circus Maximus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-champlin122_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-champlin122-47">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tacitus-annals-xv-38_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-tacitus-annals-xv-38-48">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubert_Robert_-_The_Fire_of_Rome_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2497" data-file-width="3108" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Hubert_Robert_-_The_Fire_of_Rome_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/250px-Hubert_Robert_-_The_Fire_of_Rome_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Hubert_Robert_-_The_Fire_of_Rome_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/375px-Hubert_Robert_-_The_Fire_of_Rome_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Hubert_Robert_-_The_Fire_of_Rome_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/500px-Hubert_Robert_-_The_Fire_of_Rome_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubert_Robert_-_The_Fire_of_Rome_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Fire of Rome</i> by <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Robert" title="Hubert Robert">Hubert Robert</a> (1785)</div></div></div> <p><a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>, the main ancient source for information about the fire, wrote that countless mansions, residences and temples were destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-champlin122_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-champlin122-47">[41]</a></sup> Tacitus and <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a> have both written of extensive damage to the Palatine, which has been supported by subsequent archaeological excavations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-champlin_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-champlin-49">[43]</a></sup> The fire is reported to have burned for over a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scullard_34-6"><a href="#cite_note-scullard-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>260</span></sup> It destroyed three of fourteen Roman districts and severely damaged seven more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scullard_34-7"><a href="#cite_note-scullard-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>260</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annals-xv-40_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-annals-xv-40-50">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nero_charity.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="764" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Nero_charity.jpg/220px-Nero_charity.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Nero_charity.jpg/330px-Nero_charity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Nero_charity.jpg/440px-Nero_charity.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nero_charity.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin showing Nero distributing charity to a citizen. c. 64&#8211;66.</div></div></div>\n<p>Tacitus wrote that some ancient accounts described the fire as an accident, while others had claimed that it was a plot of Nero\'s. Tacitus is the only surviving source which does not blame Nero for starting the fire; he says he is "unsure." <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, Suetonius and Cassius Dio all wrote that Nero was responsible for the fire. These accounts give several reasons for Nero\'s alleged arson like Nero\'s envy of <a href="/wiki/Priam" title="Priam">King Priam</a> and a dislike for the city\'s ancient construction. Suetonius wrote that Nero started the fire because he wanted the space to build his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_House" title="Golden House">Golden House</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[45]</a></sup> This Golden House or <i>Domus Aurea</i> included lush artificial landscapes and a 30-meter-tall statue of himself, the <a href="/wiki/Colossus_of_Nero" title="Colossus of Nero">Colossus of Nero</a>. The size of this complex is debated (from 100 to 300&#160;acres).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tacitus wrote that Nero accused Christians of starting the fire to remove suspicion from himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[49]</a></sup> According to this account, many Christians were arrested and brutally executed by "being thrown to the beasts, crucified, and being burned alive".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Suetonius and Cassius Dio alleged that Nero sang the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sack_of_Ilium" title="Sack of Ilium">Sack of Ilium</a>" in stage costume while the city burned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[52]</a></sup> The popular legend that Nero played the <a href="/wiki/Fiddle" title="Fiddle">fiddle</a> while Rome burned "is at least partly a literary construct of <a href="/wiki/Flavian_dynasty" title="Flavian dynasty">Flavian</a> propaganda [...] which looked askance on the abortive Neronian attempt to rewrite Augustan models of rule."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blackwell_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-blackwell-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2</span></sup>\n</p><p>According to Tacitus, Nero was in Antium during the fire. Upon hearing news of the fire, Nero returned to Rome to organize a relief effort, which he paid for from his own funds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annals-xv-39_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-annals-xv-39-59">[53]</a></sup> Nero\'s contributions to the relief extended to personally taking part in the search for and rescue of victims of the blaze, spending days searching the debris without even his bodyguards.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> After the fire, Nero opened his palaces to provide shelter for the homeless, and arranged for food supplies to be delivered in order to prevent starvation among the survivors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annals-xv-39_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-annals-xv-39-59">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the wake of the fire, he made a new urban development plan. Houses built after the fire were spaced out, built in brick, and faced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porticos" title="Porticos">porticos</a> on wide roads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annals-xv-43_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-annals-xv-43-60">[54]</a></sup> Nero also built a new palace complex known as the <a href="/wiki/Domus_Aurea" title="Domus Aurea">Domus Aurea</a> in an area cleared by the fire. To find the necessary funds for the reconstruction, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tributes" title="Tributes">tributes</a> were imposed on the provinces of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[55]</a></sup> The cost to rebuild Rome was immense, requiring funds the state treasury did not have. Nero devalued the <a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Roman currency</a> for the first time in the Empire\'s history. He reduced the weight of the <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a> from 84 per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_pound" title="Roman pound">Roman pound</a> to 96 (3.80&#160;grams to 3.30&#160;grams). He also reduced the silver purity from 99.5% to 93.5%&#8212;the silver weight dropping from 3.80&#160;grams to 2.97&#160;grams. Furthermore, Nero reduced the weight of the <a href="/wiki/Aureus" title="Aureus">aureus</a> from 40 per Roman pound to 45 (7.9&#160;grams to 7.2&#160;grams).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Later years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_coins_sestertius_Nero_countermark_X_Legion_Gemina.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Roman_coins_sestertius_Nero_countermark_X_Legion_Gemina.jpg/220px-Roman_coins_sestertius_Nero_countermark_X_Legion_Gemina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Roman_coins_sestertius_Nero_countermark_X_Legion_Gemina.jpg/330px-Roman_coins_sestertius_Nero_countermark_X_Legion_Gemina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Roman_coins_sestertius_Nero_countermark_X_Legion_Gemina.jpg/440px-Roman_coins_sestertius_Nero_countermark_X_Legion_Gemina.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_coins_sestertius_Nero_countermark_X_Legion_Gemina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nero, <a href="/wiki/Sestertius" title="Sestertius">Sestertius</a> with countermark "X" of <a href="/wiki/Legio_X_Gemina" title="Legio X Gemina">Legio X Gemina</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:As-Nero-Ara_pacis-RIC_0562.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="276" data-file-width="572" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/As-Nero-Ara_pacis-RIC_0562.jpg/220px-As-Nero-Ara_pacis-RIC_0562.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/As-Nero-Ara_pacis-RIC_0562.jpg/330px-As-Nero-Ara_pacis-RIC_0562.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/As-Nero-Ara_pacis-RIC_0562.jpg/440px-As-Nero-Ara_pacis-RIC_0562.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:As-Nero-Ara_pacis-RIC_0562.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nero coin, c. 66. <a href="/wiki/Ara_Pacis" title="Ara Pacis">Ara Pacis</a> on the reverse.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 65<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>, <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Calpurnius_Piso" title="Gaius Calpurnius Piso">Gaius Calpurnius Piso</a>, a Roman statesman, organized a <a href="/wiki/Pisonian_conspiracy" title="Pisonian conspiracy">conspiracy against Nero</a> with the help of Subrius Flavus and Sulpicius Asper, a tribune and a centurion of the Praetorian Guard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[57]</a></sup> According to Tacitus, many conspirators wished to "rescue the state" from the emperor and restore the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[58]</a></sup> The freedman Milichus discovered the conspiracy and reported it to Nero\'s secretary, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epaphroditos" title="Epaphroditos">Epaphroditos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup> As a result, the conspiracy failed and its members were executed including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Annaeus_Lucanus" title="Marcus Annaeus Lucanus">Lucan</a>, the poet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[60]</a></sup> Nero\'s previous advisor <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a> was accused by Natalis; he denied the charges but was still ordered to commit suicide as by this point he had fallen out of favor with Nero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nero was said to have kicked Poppaea to death in 65<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>, before she could have his second child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[62]</a></sup> Modern historians, noting the probable biases of Suetonius, Tacitus, and Cassius Dio, and the likely absence of eyewitnesses to such an event, propose that Poppaea may have died after miscarriage or in childbirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[63]</a></sup> Nero went into deep mourning; Poppaea was given a sumptuous <a href="/wiki/State_funeral" title="State funeral">state funeral</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_cult_(ancient_Rome)#Divus,_deus_and_the_numen" title="Imperial cult (ancient Rome)">divine honors</a>, and was promised a temple for her cult. A year\'s importation of incense was burned at the funeral. Her body was not cremated, as would have been strictly customary, but embalmed after the Egyptian manner and entombed; it is not known where.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_revolt_of_Vindex_and_Galba_and_the_death_of_Nero">The revolt of Vindex and Galba and the death of Nero</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The revolt of Vindex and Galba and the death of Nero">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nero_Palatino_Inv618.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2300" data-file-width="1715" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nero_Palatino_Inv618.jpg/200px-Nero_Palatino_Inv618.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nero_Palatino_Inv618.jpg/300px-Nero_Palatino_Inv618.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nero_Palatino_Inv618.jpg/400px-Nero_Palatino_Inv618.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nero_Palatino_Inv618.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A marble bust of Nero, Antiquarium of the <a href="/wiki/Palatine" title="Palatine">Palatine</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In March 68, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vindex" title="Vindex">Gaius Julius Vindex</a>, the governor of <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Lugdunensis" title="Gallia Lugdunensis">Gallia Lugdunensis</a>, rebelled against Nero\'s tax policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cassius-22_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cassius-22-71">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[66]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Virginius_Rufus" title="Lucius Virginius Rufus">Lucius Verginius Rufus</a>, the governor of <a href="/wiki/Germania_Superior" title="Germania Superior">Germania Superior</a>, was ordered to put down Vindex\'s rebellion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cassiusdio-lxiii-24_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-cassiusdio-lxiii-24-73">[67]</a></sup> In an attempt to gain support from outside his own province, Vindex called upon <a href="/wiki/Galba" title="Galba">Servius Sulpicius Galba</a>, the governor of <a href="/wiki/Hispania_Tarraconensis" title="Hispania Tarraconensis">Hispania Tarraconensis</a>, to join the rebellion and further, to declare himself emperor in opposition to Nero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plutarch-galba-5_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plutarch-galba-5-74">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Vesontio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Battle of Vesontio (page does not exist)">Battle of Vesontio</a> in May 68, Verginius\' forces easily defeated those of Vindex and the latter committed suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cassiusdio-lxiii-24_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-cassiusdio-lxiii-24-73">[67]</a></sup> However, after putting down this one rebel, Verginius\' legions attempted to proclaim their own commander as Emperor. Verginius refused to act against Nero, but the discontent of the legions of Germany and the continued opposition of Galba in Spain did not bode well for him.\n</p><p>While Nero had retained some control of the situation, support for Galba increased despite his being officially declared a public enemy ("hostis publicus"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[69]</a></sup>). The prefect of the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nymphidius_Sabinus" title="Nymphidius Sabinus">Gaius Nymphidius Sabinus</a>, also abandoned his allegiance to the Emperor and came out in support of Galba.\n</p><p>In response, Nero fled Rome with the intention of going to the port of <a href="/wiki/Ostia_Antica" title="Ostia Antica">Ostia</a> and, from there, to take a fleet to one of the still-loyal eastern provinces. According to Suetonius, Nero abandoned the idea when some army officers openly refused to obey his commands, responding with a line from <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i>: "Is it so dreadful a thing then to die?" Nero then toyed with the idea of fleeing to <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthia</a>, throwing himself upon the mercy of Galba, or appealing to the people and begging them to pardon him for his past offences "and if he could not soften their hearts, to entreat them at least to allow him the prefecture of Egypt". Suetonius reports that the text of this speech was later found in Nero\'s writing desk, but that he dared not give it from fear of being torn to pieces before he could reach the Forum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-penelope.uchicago.edu_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-penelope.uchicago.edu-76">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nero returned to Rome and spent the evening in the palace. After sleeping, he awoke at about midnight to find the palace guard had left. Dispatching messages to his friends\' palace chambers for them to come, he received no answers. Upon going to their chambers personally, he found them all abandoned. When he called for a gladiator or anyone else adept with a sword to kill him, no one appeared. He cried, "Have I neither friend nor foe?" and ran out as if to throw himself into the <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-penelope.uchicago.edu_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-penelope.uchicago.edu-76">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Returning, Nero sought a place where he could hide and collect his thoughts. An imperial freedman, <a href="/wiki/Phaon_(freedman)" title="Phaon (freedman)">Phaon</a>, offered his villa, located four miles outside the city. Travelling in disguise, Nero and four loyal <a href="/wiki/Freedman" title="Freedman">freedmen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epaphroditos" title="Epaphroditos">Epaphroditos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phaon_(freedman)" title="Phaon (freedman)">Phaon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neophytus_(freedman)" title="Neophytus (freedman)">Neophytus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sporus" title="Sporus">Sporus</a>, reached the villa, where Nero ordered them to dig a grave for him.\n</p><p>At this time, a courier arrived with a report that the Senate had declared Nero a public enemy and that it was their intention to execute him by beating him to death and that armed men had been sent to apprehend him for the act to take place in the Forum. The Senate actually was still reluctant and deliberating on the right course of action as Nero was the last member of the Julio-Claudian Family. Indeed, most of the senators had served the imperial family all their lives and felt a sense of loyalty to the deified bloodline, if not to Nero himself. The men actually had the goal of returning Nero back to the Senate, where the Senate hoped to work out a compromise with the rebelling governors that would preserve Nero\'s life, so that at least a future heir to the dynasty could be produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nero, however, did not know this, and at the news brought by the courier, he prepared himself for <a href="/wiki/Forced_suicide" title="Forced suicide">suicide</a>, pacing up and down muttering <i>Qualis artifex pereo</i> ("What an artist dies in me").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[72]</a></sup> Losing his nerve, he begged one of his companions to set an example by killing himself first. At last, the sound of approaching horsemen drove Nero to face the end. However, he still could not bring himself to take his own life but instead he forced his private secretary, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epaphroditos" title="Epaphroditos">Epaphroditos</a>, to perform the task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[73]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galleria_Borghese2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Galleria_Borghese2.jpg/220px-Galleria_Borghese2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Galleria_Borghese2.jpg/330px-Galleria_Borghese2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Galleria_Borghese2.jpg/440px-Galleria_Borghese2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galleria_Borghese2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Villa Borghese, Rome, houses the <a href="/wiki/Galleria_Borghese" title="Galleria Borghese">Galleria Borghese</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_select_collection_of_views_and_ruins_in_Rome_and_its_vicinity_-_recently_executed_from_drawings_made_upon_the_spot_(1815)_(14592716650).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2502" data-file-width="1802" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/A_select_collection_of_views_and_ruins_in_Rome_and_its_vicinity_-_recently_executed_from_drawings_made_upon_the_spot_%281815%29_%2814592716650%29.jpg/200px-A_select_collection_of_views_and_ruins_in_Rome_and_its_vicinity_-_recently_executed_from_drawings_made_upon_the_spot_%281815%29_%2814592716650%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/A_select_collection_of_views_and_ruins_in_Rome_and_its_vicinity_-_recently_executed_from_drawings_made_upon_the_spot_%281815%29_%2814592716650%29.jpg/300px-A_select_collection_of_views_and_ruins_in_Rome_and_its_vicinity_-_recently_executed_from_drawings_made_upon_the_spot_%281815%29_%2814592716650%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/A_select_collection_of_views_and_ruins_in_Rome_and_its_vicinity_-_recently_executed_from_drawings_made_upon_the_spot_%281815%29_%2814592716650%29.jpg/400px-A_select_collection_of_views_and_ruins_in_Rome_and_its_vicinity_-_recently_executed_from_drawings_made_upon_the_spot_%281815%29_%2814592716650%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_select_collection_of_views_and_ruins_in_Rome_and_its_vicinity_-_recently_executed_from_drawings_made_upon_the_spot_(1815)_(14592716650).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1815 illustration of the alleged tomb of Nero; actually tomb of proconsul <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Caius_Vibius_Marianus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Caius Vibius Marianus (page does not exist)">Caius Vibius Marianus</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>When one of the horsemen entered and saw that Nero was dying, he attempted to stop the bleeding, but efforts to save Nero\'s life were unsuccessful. Nero\'s final words were "Too late! This is fidelity!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-80">[74]</a></sup> He died on 9 June 68, the anniversary of the death of Octavia, and was buried in the Mausoleum of the Domitii Ahenobarbi, in what is now the <a href="/wiki/Villa_Borghese" title="Villa Borghese">Villa Borghese</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pincian_Hill" title="Pincian Hill">Pincian Hill</a>) area of Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-80">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With his death, the <a href="/wiki/Julio-Claudian_dynasty" title="Julio-Claudian dynasty">Julio-Claudian dynasty</a> ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agrippina_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-agrippina-81">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>19</span></sup> When news of his death reached Rome, the Senate posthumously declared Nero a public enemy to appease the coming Galba (as the Senate had initially declared Galba as a public enemy) and proclaimed Galba the new emperor. Chaos would ensue in the <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Four_Emperors" title="Year of the Four Emperors">year of the Four Emperors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tacitus-histories-I.2_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-tacitus-histories-I.2-82">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_Nero">After Nero</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: After Nero">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nero_Redivivus_legend" title="Nero Redivivus legend">Nero Redivivus legend</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Nero" title="Pseudo-Nero">Pseudo-Nero</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nero-nancy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="824" data-file-width="633" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Nero-nancy.jpg/190px-Nero-nancy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Nero-nancy.jpg/285px-Nero-nancy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Nero-nancy.jpg/380px-Nero-nancy.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nero-nancy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Apotheosis" title="Apotheosis">Apotheosis</a> of Nero, c. after 68. Artwork portraying Nero rising to divine status after his death.</div></div></div>\n<p>According to Suetonius and Cassius Dio, the people of Rome celebrated the death of Nero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[78]</a></sup> Tacitus, though, describes a more complicated political environment. Tacitus mentions that Nero\'s death was welcomed by Senators, nobility and the upper class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-histories-i-4_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-histories-i-4-85">[79]</a></sup> The lower-class, slaves, frequenters of the arena and the theater, and "those who were supported by the famous excesses of Nero", on the other hand, were upset with the news.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-histories-i-4_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-histories-i-4-85">[79]</a></sup> Members of the military were said to have mixed feelings, as they had allegiance to Nero, but had been bribed to overthrow him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tacitus-histories-I.5_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-tacitus-histories-I.5-86">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Eastern sources, namely Philostratus II and <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Tyana" title="Apollonius of Tyana">Apollonius of Tyana</a>, mention that Nero\'s death was mourned as he "restored the liberties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Greece" title="Roman Greece">Hellas</a> with a wisdom and moderation quite alien to his character"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[81]</a></sup> and that he "held our liberties in his hand and respected them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Modern scholarship generally holds that, while the Senate and more well-off individuals welcomed Nero\'s death, the general populace was "loyal to the end and beyond, for Otho and Vitellius both thought it worthwhile to appeal to their nostalgia."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nero\'s name was erased from some monuments, in what Edward Champlin regards as an "outburst of private zeal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[84]</a></sup> Many portraits of Nero were reworked to represent other figures; according to Eric R. Varner, over fifty such images survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pollini_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-pollini-91">[85]</a></sup> This reworking of images is often explained as part of the way in which the memory of disgraced emperors was condemned posthumously<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[86]</a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Damnatio_memoriae" title="Damnatio memoriae">damnatio memoriae</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pollini_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-pollini-91">[85]</a></sup> Champlin, however, doubts that the practice is necessarily negative and notes that some continued to create images of Nero long after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The civil war during the <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Four_Emperors" title="Year of the Four Emperors">year of the Four Emperors</a> was described by ancient historians as a troubling period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tacitus-histories-I.2_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-tacitus-histories-I.2-82">[76]</a></sup> According to Tacitus, this instability was rooted in the fact that emperors could no longer rely on the perceived legitimacy of the imperial bloodline, as Nero and those before him could.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-histories-i-4_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-histories-i-4-85">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Galba" title="Galba">Galba</a> began his short reign with the execution of many of Nero\'s allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[88]</a></sup> One such notable enemy included <a href="/wiki/Nymphidius_Sabinus" title="Nymphidius Sabinus">Nymphidius Sabinus</a>, who claimed to be the son of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Caligula" title="Caligula">Caligula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Otho" title="Otho">Otho</a> overthrew Galba. Otho was said to be liked by many soldiers because he had been a friend of Nero\'s and resembled him somewhat in temperament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup> It was said that the common Roman hailed Otho as Nero himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suetonius-otho-7_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-suetonius-otho-7-97">[91]</a></sup> Otho used "Nero" as a surname and reerected many statues to Nero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suetonius-otho-7_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-suetonius-otho-7-97">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vitellius" title="Vitellius">Vitellius</a> overthrew Otho. Vitellius began his reign with a large funeral for Nero complete with songs written by Nero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Nero\'s suicide in 68, there was a widespread belief, especially in the eastern provinces, that he was not dead and somehow would return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup> This belief came to be known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nero_Redivivus_Legend" title="Nero Redivivus Legend">Nero Redivivus Legend</a>.\n</p><p>The legend of Nero\'s return lasted for hundreds of years after Nero\'s death. <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> wrote of the legend as a popular belief in 422.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-augustine_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-augustine-100">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At least <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudo-Neros" title="Pseudo-Neros">three Nero imposters</a> emerged leading rebellions. The first, who sang and played the cithara or lyre and whose face was similar to that of the dead emperor, appeared in 69 during the reign of Vitellius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tacitus-histories-II.8_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-tacitus-histories-II.8-101">[95]</a></sup> After persuading some to recognize him, he was captured and executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tacitus-histories-II.8_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-tacitus-histories-II.8-101">[95]</a></sup> Sometime during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Titus" title="Titus">Titus</a> (79&#8211;81), another impostor appeared in Asia and sang to the accompaniment of the lyre and looked like Nero but he, too, was killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup> Twenty years after Nero\'s death, during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Domitian" title="Domitian">Domitian</a>, there was a third pretender.
