He was supported by the Parthians, who only reluctantly gave him up,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup> and the matter almost came to war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tacitus-histories-I.2_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-tacitus-histories-I.2-82">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_conflicts">Military conflicts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Military conflicts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Boudicca.27s_uprising"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Boudicca\'s_uprising">Boudicca\'s uprising</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Boudicca\'s uprising">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In Britannia in 59<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>, <a href="/wiki/Prasutagus" title="Prasutagus">Prasutagus</a>, leader of the <a href="/wiki/Iceni" title="Iceni">Iceni</a> tribe, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Client_king" title="Client king">client king</a> of Rome\'s during Claudius\' reign, died. The client state arrangement was unlikely to survive the death of the former Emperor. Prasutagus\' will leaving control of the Iceni to his wife <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boudicca" title="Boudicca">Boudicca</a> was denied, and, when <a href="/wiki/Catus_Decianus" title="Catus Decianus">Catus Decianus</a> scourged Boudicca and raped her daughters, the Iceni revolted. They were joined by the <a href="/wiki/Trinovantes" title="Trinovantes">Trinovantes</a> tribe, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boudicca#Boudicca\'s_uprising" title="Boudicca">their uprising</a> became the most significant provincial rebellion of the 1st century<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shotter_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-shotter-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>32</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scullard_34-8"><a href="#cite_note-scullard-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>254</span></sup>\nUnder Boudicca the towns of Camulodunum (Colchester), Londinium  (London) and Verulamium (St Albans) were burned and a substantial body of legion infantry destroyed. The governor of the province <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Suetonius_Paulinus" title="Gaius Suetonius Paulinus">Gaius Suetonius Paulinus</a> assembled his remaining forces and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Watling_Street" title="Battle of Watling Street">defeated the Britons</a> and restored order but for a while Nero considered abandoning the province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Alpinus_Classicianus" title="Gaius Julius Alpinus Classicianus">Julius Classicianus</a> replaced Decianus as <a href="/wiki/Procurator_(Ancient_Rome)" title="Procurator (Ancient Rome)">procurator</a>. Classicianus advised Nero to replace Paulinus, who continued to punish the population even after the rebellion was over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scullard_34-9"><a href="#cite_note-scullard-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>265</span></sup>\n</p><p>Nero decided to adopt a more lenient approach to governing the province, and appointed a new governor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petronius_Turpilianus" title="Petronius Turpilianus">Petronius Turpilianus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shotter_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-shotter-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>33</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_with_Parthia">Peace with Parthia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Peace with Parthia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Parthian_War_of_58%E2%80%9363" title="Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 58&#8211;63">Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 58&#8211;63</a></div>\n<p>Nero began preparing for war in the early years of his reign, after the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian</a> king <a href="/wiki/Vologases_I_of_Parthia" title="Vologases I of Parthia">Vologeses</a> set his brother <a href="/wiki/Tiridates_I_of_Armenia" title="Tiridates I of Armenia">Tiridates</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Armenia" title="Roman Armenia">Armenian</a> throne. Around 57<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span> and 58<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domitius_Corbulo" title="Domitius Corbulo">Domitius Corbulo</a> and his legions advanced on Tiridates and captured the Armenian capital <a href="/wiki/Artaxata" title="Artaxata">Artaxata</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tigranes_VI_of_Armenia" title="Tigranes VI of Armenia">Tigranes</a> was chosen to replace Tiridates on the Armenian throne. When Tigranes attacked <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a>, Nero had to send further legions to defend Armenia and Syria from Parthia.\n</p><p>The Roman victory came at a time when the Parthians were troubled by revolts; when this was dealt with they were able to devote resources to the Armenian situation. A Roman army under Paetus surrendered under humiliating circumstances and though both Roman and Parthian forces withdrew from Armenia, it was under Parthian control. The triumphal arch for Corbulo\'s earlier victory was part-built when Parthian envoys arrived in 63 AD to discuss treaties.     \nGiven <i>imperium</i> over the eastern regions, Corbulo organised his forces for an invasion but was met by this Parthian delegation. An agreement was thereafter reached with the Parthians: Rome would recognize Tiridates as king of Armenia, only if he agreed to receive his <a href="/wiki/Diadem" title="Diadem">diadem</a> from Nero. A coronation ceremony was held in Italy 66<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>. Dio reports that Tiridates said "I have come to you, my God, worshiping you as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mithras" title="Mithras">Mithras</a>." Shotter says this parallels other divine designations that were commonly applied to Nero in the East including "The New <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>" and "The New Sun." After the coronation, friendly relations were established between Rome and the eastern kingdoms of Parthia and Armenia. Artaxata was temporarily renamed Neroneia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scullard_34-10"><a href="#cite_note-scullard-34">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>265&#8211;66</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shotter_15-8"><a href="#cite_note-shotter-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>35</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_First_Jewish_War">The First Jewish War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: The First Jewish War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Jewish-Roman_War" title="First Jewish-Roman War">First Jewish-Roman War</a></div>\n<p>In 66, there was a Jewish revolt in Judea stemming from Greek and Jewish religious tension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup> In 67, Nero dispatched <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a> to restore order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup> This revolt was eventually put down in 70, after Nero\'s death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[101]</a></sup> This revolt is famous for Romans breaching the walls of Jerusalem and destroying the Second <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temple_of_Jerusalem" title="Temple of Jerusalem">Temple of Jerusalem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pursuits">Pursuits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Pursuits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Nero studied poetry, music, painting and sculpture. He both sang and played the <i><a href="/wiki/Cithara" title="Cithara">cithara</a></i> (a type of <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyre</a>). Many of these disciplines were standard education for the Roman elite, but Nero\'s devotion to music exceeded what was socially acceptable for a Roman of his class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-griffin_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-griffin-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>41&#8211;2</span></sup> Ancient sources were critical of Nero\'s emphasis on the arts, chariot-racing and athletics. Pliny described Nero as an "actor-emperor" (<i>scaenici imperatoris</i>) and Suetonius wrote that he was "carried away by a craze for popularity...since he was acclaimed as the equal of Apollo in music and of the Sun in driving a chariot, he had planned to emulate the exploits of Hercules as well."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-champlin_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-champlin-49">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>53</span></sup>\n</p><p>In 67 <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span> Nero participated in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympics</a>. He had bribed organizers to postpone the games for a year so he could participate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup> and artistic competitions were added to the athletic events. Nero won every contest in which he was a competitor.  During the games Nero sang and played his lyre on stage, acted in tragedies and raced chariots. He won a 10-horse chariot race, despite being thrown from the chariot and leaving the race. He was crowned on the basis that he would have won if he had completed the race. After he died a year later, his name was removed from the list of winners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup>  Champlin writes that though Nero\'s participation "effectively stifled true competition, [Nero] seems to have been oblivious of reality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-champlin_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-champlin-49">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>54&#8211;5</span></sup>\n</p><p>Nero established the Neronian games in 60<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>. Modeled on Greek style games, these games included "music" "gymnastic" and "questrian" contents. According to Suetonius the gymnastic contests were held in the Saepta area of the <a href="/wiki/Campus_Martius" title="Campus Martius">Campus Martius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-champlin_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-champlin-49">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>288</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The history of Nero\'s reign is problematic in that no historical sources survived that were contemporary with Nero. These first histories, while they still existed, were described as biased and fantastical, either overly critical or praising of Nero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup> The original sources were also said to contradict on a number of events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup> Nonetheless, these lost primary sources were the basis of surviving secondary and tertiary histories on Nero written by the next generations of historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup> A few of the contemporary historians are known by name. <a href="/wiki/Fabius_Rusticus" title="Fabius Rusticus">Fabius Rusticus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cluvius_Rufus" title="Cluvius Rufus">Cluvius Rufus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> all wrote condemning histories on Nero that are now lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup> There were also pro-Nero histories, but it is unknown who wrote them or for what deeds Nero was praised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The bulk of what is known of Nero comes from <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a>, who were all of the senatorial class. Tacitus and Suetonius wrote their histories on Nero over fifty years after his death, while Cassius Dio wrote his history over 150 years after Nero\'s death. These sources contradict one another on a number of events in Nero\'s life including the death of <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a>, the death of <a href="/wiki/Agrippina_the_Younger" title="Agrippina the Younger">Agrippina</a>, and the Roman fire of 64, but they are consistent in their condemnation of Nero.\n</p><p>A handful of other sources also add a limited and varying perspective on Nero. Few surviving sources paint Nero in a favourable light. Some sources, though, portray him as a competent emperor who was popular with the Roman people, especially in the east.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Cassius Dio</dt></dl>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a> (c. 155&#8211;229) was the son of <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Apronianus" title="Cassius Apronianus">Cassius Apronianus</a>, a Roman senator. He passed the greater part of his life in public service. He was a senator under <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a> and governor of Smyrna after the death of <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a>; and afterwards suffect consul around 205, and also proconsul in Africa and Pannonia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Books 61&#8211;63 of Dio\'s <i>Roman History</i> describe the reign of Nero. Only fragments of these books remain and what does remain was abridged and altered by <a href="/wiki/John_Xiphilinus" title="John Xiphilinus">John Xiphilinus</a>, an 11th-century monk.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Dio Chrysostom</dt></dl>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Dio_Chrysostom" title="Dio Chrysostom">Dio Chrysostom</a> (c. 40&#8211;120), a Greek philosopher and historian, wrote the Roman people were very happy with Nero and would have allowed him to rule indefinitely. They longed for his rule once he was gone and embraced imposters when they appeared:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Indeed the truth about this has not come out even yet; for so far as the rest of his subjects were concerned, there was nothing to prevent his continuing to be Emperor for all time, seeing that even now everybody wishes he were still alive. And the great majority do believe that he still is, although in a certain sense he has died not once but often along with those who had been firmly convinced that he was still alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<dl><dt>Epictetus</dt></dl>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Epictetus" title="Epictetus">Epictetus</a> (c. 55&#8211;135) was the slave to Nero\'s scribe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epaphroditos" title="Epaphroditos">Epaphroditos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[111]</a></sup> He makes a few passing negative comments on Nero\'s character in his work, but makes no remarks on the nature of his rule. He describes Nero as a spoiled, angry and unhappy man.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Josephus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1330" data-file-width="1050" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Josephus.jpg/220px-Josephus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Josephus.jpg/330px-Josephus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Josephus.jpg/440px-Josephus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Josephus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A circa 18th C woodcut of the historian <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a> (c. 37&#8211;100) who accused other historians of slandering Nero.</div></div></div>\n<dl><dt>Josephus</dt></dl>\n<p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a> (c. 37&#8211;100), while calling Nero a tyrant, was also the first to mention bias against Nero. Of other historians, he said:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>But I omit any further discourse about these affairs; for there have been a great many who have composed the history of Nero; some of which have departed from the truth of facts out of favour, as having received benefits from him; while others, out of hatred to him, and the great ill-will which they bore him, have so impudently raved against him with their lies, that they justly deserve to be condemned. Nor do I wonder at such as have told lies of Nero, since they have not in their writings preserved the truth of history as to those facts that were earlier than his time, even when the actors could have no way incurred their hatred, since those writers lived a long time after them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[112]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<dl><dt>Lucan</dt></dl>\n<p>Although more of a poet than historian, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Annaeus_Lucanus" title="Marcus Annaeus Lucanus">Lucanus</a> (c. 39&#8211;65) has one of the kindest accounts of Nero\'s rule. He writes of peace and prosperity under Nero in contrast to previous war and strife. Ironically, he was later involved in a conspiracy to overthrow Nero and was executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[113]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Philostratus</dt></dl>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Philostratus" title="Philostratus">Philostratus</a> II "the Athenian" (c. 172&#8211;250) spoke of Nero in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_of_Apollonius_Tyana" title="Life of Apollonius Tyana">Life of Apollonius Tyana</a> (Books 4&#8211;5). Although he has a generally bad or dim view of Nero, he speaks of others\' positive reception of Nero in the East.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Pliny the Elder</dt></dl>\n<p>The history of Nero by <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> (c. 24&#8211;79) did not survive. Still, there are several references to Nero in Pliny\'s <i>Natural Histories</i>. Pliny has one of the worst opinions of Nero and calls him an "enemy of mankind."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Plutarch</dt></dl>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> (c. 46&#8211;127) mentions Nero indirectly in his account of the Life of Galba and the Life of Otho, as well as in the Vision of Thespesius in Book 7 of the Moralia, where a voice orders that Nero\'s soul be transferred to a more offensive species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup> Nero is portrayed as a tyrant, but those that replace him are not described as better.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Seneca the Younger</dt></dl>\n<p>It is not surprising that <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a> (c. 4 BC&#8211;65), Nero\'s teacher and advisor, writes very well of Nero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[116]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Suetonius</dt></dl>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lives_of_the_Twelve_Caesars" title="Lives of the Twelve Caesars">Lives of the Twelve Caesars</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a> (c. 69&#8211;130) was a member of the equestrian order, and he was the head of the department of the imperial correspondence. While in this position, Suetonius started writing biographies of the emperors, accentuating the anecdotal and sensational aspects.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Tacitus</dt></dl>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Annals_(Tacitus)" title="Annals (Tacitus)">Annals (Tacitus)</a></div>\n<p>The <i>Annals</i> by <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> (c. 56&#8211;117) is the most detailed and comprehensive history on the rule of Nero, despite being incomplete after the year 66<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>. Tacitus described the rule of the Julio-Claudian emperors as generally unjust. He also thought that existing writing on them was unbalanced:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The histories of Tiberius, Caius, Claudius and Nero, while they were in power, were falsified through terror, and after their death were written under the irritation of a recent hatred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[117]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Tacitus was the son of a <a href="/wiki/Promagistrate" title="Promagistrate">procurator</a>, who married into the elite family of Agricola. He entered his political life as a senator after Nero\'s death and, by Tacitus\' own admission, owed much to Nero\'s rivals. Realising that this bias may be apparent to others, Tacitus protests that his writing is true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[118]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Girolamo Cardano</dt></dl>\n<p>In 1562 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Girolamo_Cardano" title="Girolamo Cardano">Girolamo Cardano</a> published in Basel his <i>Encomium Neronis</i>, which was one of the first historical references of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_era" title="Modern era">Modern era</a> to portray Nero in a positive light.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nero_in_Jewish_and_Christian_tradition">Nero in Jewish and Christian tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Nero in Jewish and Christian tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_tradition">Jewish tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Jewish tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>At the end of 66<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>, conflict broke out between Greeks and Jews in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> and Caesarea. According to the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a>, Nero went to Jerusalem and shot arrows in all four directions. All the arrows landed in the city. He then asked a passing child to repeat the verse he had learned that day. The child responded, "I will lay my vengeance upon Edom by the hand of my people Israel" (<a href="/wiki/Ezekiel" title="Ezekiel">Ez.</a> <a class="external text" href="http://av1611.com/kjbp/kjv-bible-text/Eze-25.html" rel="nofollow">25,14</a>). Nero became terrified, believing that God wanted the <a href="/wiki/Temple_in_Jerusalem" title="Temple in Jerusalem">Temple in Jerusalem</a> to be destroyed, but would punish the one to carry it out. Nero said, "He desires to lay waste His House and to lay the blame on me," whereupon he fled and converted to Judaism to avoid such retribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a> was then dispatched to put down the rebellion.\n</p><p>The Talmud adds that the sage <a href="/wiki/Rabbi_Meir" title="Rabbi Meir">Reb Meir Baal HaNess</a> lived in the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mishna" title="Mishna">Mishna</a>, and was a prominent supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Simon_bar_Kokhba" title="Simon bar Kokhba">Bar Kokhba</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bar_Kokhba%27s_revolt" title="Bar Kokhba\'s revolt">rebellion</a> against Roman rule. Rabbi Meir was considered one of the greatest of the <a href="/wiki/Tannaim" title="Tannaim">Tannaim</a> of the third generation (139-163). According to the Talmud, his father was a descendant of Nero who had converted to Judaism. His wife <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bruriah_daughter_of_Rabbi_Hananiah_Ben_Teradion" title="Bruriah daughter of Rabbi Hananiah Ben Teradion">Bruriah</a> is one of the few women cited in the <a href="/wiki/Gemara" title="Gemara">Gemara</a>. He is the third-most-frequently-mentioned sage in the Mishnah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[120]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Roman and Greek sources nowhere report Nero\'s alleged trip to Jerusalem or his alleged conversion to Judaism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[121]</a></sup> There is also no record of Nero having any offspring who survived infancy: his only recorded child, <a href="/wiki/Claudia_Augusta" title="Claudia Augusta">Claudia Augusta</a>, died aged 4 months.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_tradition">Christian tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Christian tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siemiradzki_Christian_Dirce.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="2273" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Siemiradzki_Christian_Dirce.jpg/400px-Siemiradzki_Christian_Dirce.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Siemiradzki_Christian_Dirce.jpg/600px-Siemiradzki_Christian_Dirce.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Siemiradzki_Christian_Dirce.jpg/800px-Siemiradzki_Christian_Dirce.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siemiradzki_Christian_Dirce.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>A Christian Dirce</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Henryk_Siemiradzki" title="Henryk Siemiradzki">Henryk Siemiradzki</a>. A Christian woman is martyred in this re-enactment of the myth of <a href="/wiki/Dirce" title="Dirce">Dirce</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siemiradski_Fackeln.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="2018" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Siemiradski_Fackeln.jpg/400px-Siemiradski_Fackeln.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Siemiradski_Fackeln.jpg/600px-Siemiradski_Fackeln.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Siemiradski_Fackeln.jpg/800px-Siemiradski_Fackeln.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siemiradski_Fackeln.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nero\'s Torches</div></div></div>\n<p>Non-Christian historian <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> describes Nero extensively torturing and executing Christians after the fire of 64.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annals-xv-44_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-annals-xv-44-9">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a> also mentions Nero punishing Christians, though he does so because they are "given to a new and mischievous superstition" and does not connect it with the fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[122]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Christian writer <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> (c. 155&#8211;230) was the first to call Nero the first persecutor of Christians. He wrote, "Examine your records. There you will find that Nero was the first that persecuted this doctrine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a> (c. 240&#8211;320) also said that Nero "first persecuted the servants of God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lactantius_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-lactantius-130">[124]</a></sup> as does <a href="/wiki/Sulpicius_Severus" title="Sulpicius Severus">Sulpicius Severus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[125]</a></sup> However, Suetonius writes that, "since the Jews constantly made disturbances at the instigation of Chrestus, the [emperor <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a>] expelled them from Rome" ("<i>Iudaeos impulsore Chresto assidue tumultuantis Roma expulit</i>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[126]</a></sup> These expelled "Jews" may have been early Christians, although Suetonius is not explicit. Nor is the Bible explicit, calling Aquila of Pontus and his wife, Priscilla, both expelled from Italy at the time, "Jews".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[127]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Martyrdoms_of_Peter_and_Paul">Martyrdoms of Peter and Paul</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Martyrdoms of Peter and Paul">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The first text to suggest that Nero ordered the execution of an apostle is a letter by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_I" title="Pope Clement I">Clement</a> to the Corinthians traditionally dated to around 96 A.D.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Champlin2009_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Champlin2009-134">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>123&#8211;</span></sup> The apocryphal <a href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Isaiah" title="Ascension of Isaiah">Ascension of Isaiah</a>, a Christian writing from the 2nd century, says, "the slayer of his mother, who himself (even) this king, will persecute the plant which the Twelve Apostles of the Beloved have planted. Of the Twelve one will be delivered into his hands"; this is interpreted as referring to Nero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ascension_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-ascension-135">[129]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">Bishop</a> <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a> of <a href="/wiki/Caesarea" title="Caesarea">Caesarea</a> (c. 275&#8211;339) was the first to write explicitly that Paul was beheaded in Rome during the reign of Nero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[130]</a></sup> He states that Nero\'s persecution led to Peter and Paul\'s deaths, but that Nero did not give any specific orders. However, several other accounts going back to the 1st century have Paul surviving his two years in Rome and travelling to <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, before facing trial in Rome again prior to his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[131]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Peter is first said to have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross_of_St._Peter" title="Cross of St. Peter">crucified upside-down</a> in Rome during Nero\'s reign (but not by Nero) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apocryphal" title="Apocryphal">apocryphal</a> <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Peter" title="Acts of Peter">Acts of Peter</a> (c. 200).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[132]</a></sup> The account ends with Paul still alive and Nero abiding by God\'s command not to persecute any more Christians.\n</p><p>By the 4th century, a number of writers were stating that Nero killed Peter and Paul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[133]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Antichrist">The Antichrist</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: The Antichrist">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Antichrist" title="Antichrist">Antichrist</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Beast_(Revelation)" title="The Beast (Revelation)">The Beast (Revelation)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Number_of_the_Beast" title="Number of the Beast">Number of the Beast</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sibylline_Oracles" title="Sibylline Oracles">Sibylline Oracles</a>, Book 5 and 8, written in the 2nd century, speak of Nero returning and bringing destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GriffinGriffin2002_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-GriffinGriffin2002-141">[135]</a></sup> Within Christian communities, these writings, along with others,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[136]</a></sup> fueled the belief that Nero would return as the Antichrist. In 310, <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a> wrote that Nero "suddenly disappeared, and even the burial place of that noxious wild beast was nowhere to be seen. This has led some persons of extravagant imagination to suppose that, having been conveyed to a distant region, he is still reserved alive; and to him they apply the Sibylline verses". Lactantius maintains that it is not right to believe this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lactantius_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-lactantius-130">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Champlin2009_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Champlin2009-134">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>20&#8211;</span></sup>\n</p><p>In 422, <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> wrote about 2 Thessalonians 2:1&#8211;11, where he believed Paul mentioned the coming of the Antichrist. Although he rejects the theory, Augustine mentions that many Christians believed Nero was the Antichrist or would return as the Antichrist. He wrote, "so that in saying, \'For the mystery of iniquity doth already work,\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[137]</a></sup> he alluded to Nero, whose deeds already seemed to be as the deeds of Antichrist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-augustine_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-augustine-100">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some modern biblical scholars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[139]</a></sup> such as Delbert Hillers (<a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a>) of the <a href="/wiki/American_Schools_of_Oriental_Research" title="American Schools of Oriental Research">American Schools of Oriental Research</a> and the editors of the <i>Oxford Study Bible</i> and <i>Harper Collins Study Bible</i>, contend that the number <a href="/wiki/Number_of_the_Beast" title="Number of the Beast">666</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a> is a code for Nero,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup> a view that is also supported in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> Biblical commentaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[142]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" </td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Nero_in_popular_culture" title="Nero in popular culture">Nero in popular culture</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Classical Latin spelling and <a href="/wiki/Latin_spelling_and_pronunciation" title="Latin spelling and pronunciation">reconstructed Classical Latin pronunciation</a> of the names of Nero:\n<ol><li><span class="square-capitals" lang="la" style="font-family: Cardo, \'Everson Mono\', \'GNU Unifont\', Junicode; text-transform: uppercase; font-size: 73%;">LVCIVS DOMITIVS AHENOBARBVS</span><br/><small>IPA:&#160;</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[\'lu&#720;.ki.&#650;s d&#596;\'m&#618;.ti.&#650;s a.e&#720;.n&#596;\'bar.b&#650;s]</a></span></li>\n<li><span class="square-capitals" lang="la" style="font-family: Cardo, \'Everson Mono\', \'GNU Unifont\', Junicode; text-transform: uppercase; font-size: 73%;">NERO CLAVDIVS CAESAR AVGVSTVS GERMANICVS</span><br/><small>IPA:&#160;</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[\'n&#603;.ro&#720; \'klau&#815;.di.&#650;s &#712;kae&#815;.sar au&#815;&#712;g&#650;s.t&#650;s g&#603;r\'ma&#720;.n&#618;.k&#650;s]</a></span></li></ol>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Tacitus wrote the following about Agrippina\'s marriage to Claudius: "From this moment the country was transformed. Complete obedience was accorded to a woman&#8212;and not a woman like Messalina who toyed with national affairs. This was a rigorous, almost masculine, despotism. In public, Agrippina was austere and often arrogant. Her private life was chaste&#8212;unless power was to be gained. Her passion to acquire money was unbounded; she wanted it as a stepping stone to supremacy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shotter_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-shotter-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>11</span></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to <i>The Oxford Encyclopedia of Greece and Rome</i> Nero was adopted in 50 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEGR_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-OEGR-11">[10]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For further information see <a href="/wiki/Adoption_in_ancient_Rome" title="Adoption in ancient Rome">adoption in Rome</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Suetonius wrote "It is commonly agreed that Claudius was killed by poison. There is, however, disagreement as to where and by whom it was administered. Some record that, when he was at a feast with priests on the citadel, it was given to him by his taster, the eunuch Halotus, others that it was given him at a family dinner by Agrippina herself, offering him the drug in a dish of mushrooms, a kind of food to which he was very partial...His death was concealed until all arrangements were in place with regard to his successor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-24">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>193</span></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sources describe Acte as a slave girl (Shotter) and a freedwoman (Champlin and Scullard).</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-Jarus-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Jarus_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Jarus_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Jarus_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Jarus, Owen (2013-10-08). <a class="external text" href="https://www.livescience.com/40277-emperor-nero-facts.html" rel="nofollow">"Emperor Nero: Facts &amp; Biography"</a>. <i>Live Science</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. (Gittin 56a)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Suetonius states that Nero committed suicide in Suetonius, <i>The Lives of Twelve Caesars</i>, Life of Nero <a class="external text" href="http://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/E/Roman/Texts/Suetonius/12Caesars/Nero*.html#49" rel="nofollow">49</a>; Sulpicius Severus, who possibly used Tacitus\' lost fragments as a source, reports that it was uncertain whether Nero committed suicide, Sulpicius Severus, <i>Chronica</i> II.29, also see T.D. Barnes, "The Fragments of Tacitus\' Histories", <i>Classical Philology</i> (1977), p. 228.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Galba" title="Galba">Galba</a> criticized the excesses (<i>luxuria</i>) of Nero\'s public and private spending. See Kragelund, Patrick, "Nero\'s Luxuria, in Tacitus and in the Octavia", in <i>The Classical Quarterly</i>, 2000, pp. 494&#8211;515. Kragelund is citing Tacitus, <i>Annals</i> I.16</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">References to Nero\'s matricide appear in the <i><a href="/wiki/Sibylline_Oracles" title="Sibylline Oracles">Sibylline Oracles</a></i> 5.490&#8211;520, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>\'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canterbury_Tales" title="Canterbury Tales">Canterbury Tales</a></i> <a href="/wiki/The_Monk%27s_Tale" title="The Monk\'s Tale">The Monk\'s Tale</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i> 3.ii.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/L/Roman/Texts/Suetonius/12Caesars/Nero*.html" rel="nofollow">"Suetonius &#8226; Vita Neronis"</a>. <i>penelope.uchicago.edu</i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=penelope.uchicago.edu&amp;rft.atitle=Suetonius+%E2%80%A2+Vita+Neronis&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fpenelope.uchicago.edu%2FThayer%2FL%2FRoman%2FTexts%2FSuetonius%2F12Caesars%2FNero%2A.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANero"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-annals-xv-44-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-annals-xv-44_9-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-annals-xv-44_9-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Tacitus, <i>Annals</i>.
